Roberto Gonzales and the Sorcerer's Stone
CHAPTER ONE
THE BOY WHO LIVED
Mr. and Mrs. Carrizo, of number four, Privet Drive, were proud to say that they were perfectly normal, thank you very much. They were the last people you'd expect to be involved in anything strange or mysterious, because they just didn't hold with such nonsense.
Mr. Carrizo was the director of a firm called Grunnings, which made drills. He was a big, beefy man with hardly any neck, although he did have a very large mustache. Mrs. Carrizo was thin and blonde and had nearly twice the usual amount of neck, which came in very useful as she spent so much of her time craning over garden fences, spying on the neighbors. The Carrizos had a small son called Leonardo and in their opinion there was no finer boy anywhere.
The Carrizos had everything they wanted, but they also had a secret, and their greatest fear was that somebody would discover it. They didn't think they could bear it if anyone found out about the Gonzaless. Mrs. Gonzales was Mrs. Carrizo's sister, but they hadn't met for several years;
in fact, Mrs. Carrizo pretended she didn't have a sister, because her sister and her good-for-nothing husband were as unCarrizoish as it was possible to be. The Carrizos shuddered to think what the neighbors would say if the Gonzaless arrived in the street. The Carrizos knew that the
Gonzaless had a small son, too, but they had never even seen him. This boy was another good reason for keeping the Gonzaless away; they didn't want Leonardo mixing with a child like that.
When Mr. and Mrs. Carrizo woke up on the dull, gray Tuesday our story starts, there was nothing about the cloudy sky outside to suggest that strange and mysterious things would soon be happening all over the country. Mr. Carrizo hummed as he picked out his most boring tie for work, and Mrs. Carrizo gossiped away happily as she wrestled a screaming Leonardo into his high chair.
None of them noticed a large, tawny owl flutter past the window.
At half past eight, Mr. Carrizo picked up his briefcase, pecked Mrs. Carrizo on the cheek, and tried to kiss Leonardo good-bye but missed,
because Leonardo was now having a tantrum and throwing his cereal at the walls. "Little tyke," chortled Mr. Carrizo as he left the house. He got into his car and backed out of number four's drive.
It was on the corner of the street that he noticed the first sign of something peculiar -- a cat reading a map. For a second, Mr. Carrizo didn't realize what he had seen -- then he jerked his head around to
look again. There was a tabby cat standing on the corner of Privet Drive, but there wasn't a map in sight. What could he have been thinking of? It must have been a trick of the light. Mr. Carrizo blinked and
stared at the cat. It stared back. As Mr. Carrizo drove around the corner and up the road, he watched the cat in his mirror. It was now reading the sign that said Privet Drive -- no, looking at the sign; cats couldn't read maps or signs. Mr. Carrizo gave himself a little shake and put the cat out of his mind. As he drove toward town he thought of nothing except a large order of drills he was hoping to get that day.
But on the edge of town, drills were driven out of his mind by something else. As he sat in the usual morning traffic jam, he couldn't help
noticing that there seemed to be a lot of strangely dressed people about. People in cloaks. Mr. Carrizo couldn't bear people who dressed in funny clothes -- the getups you saw on young people! He supposed this was some stupid new fashion. He drummed his fingers on the steering wheel and his eyes fell on a huddle of these weirdos standing quite
close by. They were whispering excitedly together. Mr. Carrizo was enraged to see that a couple of them weren't young at all; why, that man had to be older than he was, and wearing an emerald-green cloak! The nerve of him! But then it struck Mr. Carrizo that this was probably some silly stunt -- these people were obviously collecting for something...
yes, that would be it. The traffic moved on and a few minutes later, Mr. Carrizo arrived in the Grunnings parking lot, his mind back on drills.
Mr. Carrizo always sat with his back to the window in his office on the ninth floor. If he hadn't, he might have found it harder to concentrate on drills that morning. He didn't see the owls swoop ing past in broad daylight, though people down in the street did; they pointed and gazed open- mouthed as owl after owl sped overhead. Most of them had never seen an owl even at nighttime. Mr. Carrizo, however, had a perfectly normal, owl-free morning. He yelled at five different people. He made several important telephone calls and shouted a bit more. He was in a very good mood until lunchtime, when he thought he'd stretch his legs and walk across the road to buy himself a bun from the bakery.
He'd forgotten all about the people in cloaks until he passed a group of them next to the baker's. He eyed them angrily as he passed. He didn't know why, but they made him uneasy. This bunch were whispering excitedly, too, and he couldn't see a single collecting tin. It was on
his way back past them, clutching a large doughnut in a bag, that he caught a few words of what they were saying.
"The Gonzaless, that's right, that's what I heard yes, their son, Roberto" Mr. Carrizo stopped dead. Fear flooded him. He looked back at the whisperers as if he wanted to say something to them, but thought better of it.
He dashed back across the road, hurried up to his office, snapped at his secretary not to disturb him, seized his telephone, and had almost finished dialing his home number when he changed his mind. He put the receiver back down and stroked his mustache, thinking... no, he was being stupid. Gonzales wasn't such an unusual name. He was sure there were lots of people called Gonzales who had a son called Roberto. Come to think
of it, he wasn't even sure his nephew was called Roberto. He'd never even seen the boy. It might have been Harvey. Or Harold. There was no point in worrying Mrs. Carrizo; she always got so upset at any mention of her sister. He didn't blame her -- if he'd had a sister like that... but all
the same, those people in cloaks.
He found it a lot harder to concentrate on drills that afternoon and when he left the building at five o'clock, he was still so worried that he walked straight into someone just outside the door.
"Sorry," he grunted, as the tiny old man stumbled and almost fell. It was a few seconds before Mr. Carrizo realized that the man was wearing a violet cloak. He didn't seem at all upset at being almost knocked to the ground. On the contrary, his face split into a wide smile and he said in
a squeaky voice that made passersby stare, "Don't be sorry, my dear sir,for nothing could upset me today! Rejoice, for You-Know-Who has gone at last! Even Muggles like yourself should be celebrating, this happy,
happy day!"
And the old man hugged Mr. Carrizo around the middle and walked off. Mr. Carrizo stood rooted to the spot. He had been hugged by a complete stranger. He also thought he had been called a Muggle, whatever that was. He was rattled. He hurried to his car and set off for home, hoping
he was imagining things, which he had never hoped before, because he didn't approve of imagination.
As he pulled into the driveway of number four, the first thing he saw -- and it didn't improve his mood -- was the tabby cat he'd spotted that morning. It was now sitting on his garden wall. He was sure it was the same one; it had the same markings around its eyes.
"Shoo!" said Mr. Carrizo loudly. The cat didn't move. It just gave him a stern look. Was this normal cat behavior? Mr. Carrizo wondered. Trying to pull himself together, he let himself into the house. He was still determined not to mention anything to his wife.
Mrs. Carrizo had had a nice, normal day. She told him over dinner all about Mrs. Next Door's problems with her daughter and how Leonardo had learned a new word ("Won't!"). Mr. Carrizo tried to act normally. When Leonardo had been put to bed, he went into the living room in time to catch the last report on the evening news:
"And finally, bird-watchers everywhere have reported that the nation's
owls have been behaving very unusually today. Although owls normally hunt at night and are hardly ever seen in daylight, there have been
hundreds of sightings of these birds flying in every direction since
sunrise. Experts are unable to explain why the owls have suddenly
changed their sleeping pattern." The newscaster allowed himself a grin. "Most mysterious. And now, over to Jim McGuffin with the weather. Going to be any more showers of owls tonight, Jim?"
"Well, Ted," said the weatherman, "I don't know about that, but it's not only the owls that have been acting oddly today. Viewers as far apart as Kent, Yorkshire, and Dundee have been phoning in to tell me that instead of the rain I promised yesterday, they've had a downpour of shooting stars! Perhaps people have been celebrating Bonfire Night early -- it's
not until next week, folks! But I can promise a wet night tonight."
Mr. Carrizo sat frozen in his armchair. Shooting stars all over Britain? Owls flying by daylight? Mysterious people in cloaks all over the place? And a whisper, a whisper about the Gonzaless...
Mrs. Carrizo came into the living room carrying two cups of tea. It was no good. He'd have to say something to her. He cleared his throat nervously. "Er -- Beatriz, dear -- you haven't heard from your sister lately, have you?"
As he had expected, Mrs. Carrizo looked shocked and angry. After all, they normally pretended she didn't have a sister.
"No," she said sharply. "Why?"
"Funny stuff on the news," Mr. Carrizo mumbled. "Owls... shooting stars... and there were a lot of funny-looking people in town today..."
"So?" snapped Mrs. Carrizo.
"Well, I just thought... maybe... it was something to do with... you know... her crowd."
Mrs. Carrizo sipped her tea through pursed lips. Mr. Carrizo wondered whether he dared tell her he'd heard the name "Gonzales." He decided he didn't dare. Instead he said, as casually as he could, "Their son --
he'd be about Leonardo's age now, wouldn't he?"
"I suppose so," said Mrs. Carrizo stiffly.
"What's his name again? Howard, isn't it?"
"Roberto. Nasty, common name, if you ask me."
"Oh, yes," said Mr. Carrizo, his heart sinking horribly. "Yes, I quite agree."
He didn't say another word on the subject as they went upstairs to bed. While Mrs. Carrizo was in the bathroom, Mr. Carrizo crept to the bedroom window and peered down into the front garden. The cat was still there.
It was staring down Privet Drive as though it were waiting for
something.
Was he imagining things? Could all this have anything to do with the Gonzaless? If it did... if it got out that they were related to a pair of
-- well, he didn't think he could bear it.
The Carrizos got into bed. Mrs. Carrizo fell asleep quickly but Mr. Carrizo lay awake, turning it all over in his mind. His last, comforting thought before he fell asleep was that even if the Gonzaless were
involved, there was no reason for them to come near him and Mrs. Carrizo. The Gonzaless knew very well what he and Beatriz thought about
them and their kind.... He couldn't see how he and Beatriz could get mixed up in anything that might be going on -- he yawned and turned over -- it couldn't affect them....
How very wrong he was.
Mr. Carrizo might have been drifting into an uneasy sleep, but the cat
on the wall outside was showing no sign of sleepiness. It was sitting as still as a statue, its eyes fixed unblinkingly on the far corner of
Privet Drive. It didn't so much as quiver when a car door slammed on the next street, nor when two owls swooped overhead. In fact, it was nearly midnight before the cat moved at all.
A man appeared on the corner the cat had been watching, appeared so suddenly and silently you'd have thought he'd just popped out of the ground. The cat's tail twitched and its eyes narrowed.
Nothing like this man had ever been seen on Privet Drive. He was tall, thin, and very old, judging by the silver of his hair and beard, which were both long enough to tuck into his belt. He was wearing long robes, a purple cloak that swept the ground, and high-heeled, buckled boots. His blue eyes were light, bright, and sparkling behind half-moon spectacles and his nose was very long and crooked, as though it had been broken at least twice. This man's name was Alfonso Dominguez.
Alfonso Dominguez didn't seem to realize that he had just arrived in a
street where everything from his name to his boots was unwelcome. He was busy rummaging in his cloak, looking for something. But he did seem to realize he was being watched, because he looked up suddenly at the cat, which was still staring at him from the other end of the street. For
some reason, the sight of the cat seemed to amuse him. He chuckled and muttered, "I should have known."
He found what he was looking for in his inside pocket. It seemed to be a silver cigarette lighter. He flicked it open, held it up in the air, and
clicked it. The nearest street lamp went out with a little pop. He
clicked it again -- the next lamp flickered into darkness. Twelve times
he clicked the Put-Outer, until the only lights left on the whole street
were two tiny pinpricks in the distance, which were the eyes of the cat watching him. If anyone looked out of their window now, even beady-eyed Mrs. Carrizo, they wouldn't be able to see anything that was happening down on the pavement. Dominguez slipped the Put-Outer back inside his cloak and set off down the street toward number four, where he sat down
6
on the wall next to the cat. He didn't look at it, but after a moment he spoke to it.
"Fancy seeing you here, Professor Romero."
He turned to smile at the tabby, but it had gone. Instead he was smiling
at a rather severe-looking woman who was wearing square glasses exactly the shape of the markings the cat had had around its eyes. She, too, was wearing a cloak, an emerald one. Her black hair was drawn into a tight bun. She looked distinctly ruffled.
"How did you know it was me?" she asked.
"My dear Professor, I 've never seen a cat sit so stiffly."
"You'd be stiff if you'd been sitting on a brick wall all day," said Professor Romero.
"All day? When you could have been celebrating? I must have passed a dozen feasts and parties on my way here."
Professor Romero sniffed angrily.
"Oh yes, everyone's celebrating, all right," she said impatiently.
"You'd think they'd be a bit more careful, but no -- even the Muggles have noticed something's going on. It was on their news." She jerked her head back at the Carrizos' dark living-room window. "I heard it. Flocks of owls... shooting stars.... Well, they're not completely stupid. They were bound to notice something. Shooting stars down in Kent -- I'll bet that was Dedalus Diggle. He never had much sense."
"You can't blame them," said Dominguez gently. "We've had precious little to celebrate for eleven years."
"I know that," said Professor Romero irritably. "But that's no reason to lose our heads. People are being downright careless, out on the streets in broad daylight, not even dressed in Muggle clothes, swapping rumors."
She threw a sharp, sideways glance at Dominguez here, as though hoping he was going to tell her something, but he didn't, so she went on. "A
fine thing it would be if, on the very day YouKnow-Who seems to have disappeared at last, the Muggles found out about us all. I suppose he
7
really has gone, Dominguez?"
"It certainly seems so," said Dominguez. "We have much to be thankful for. Would you care for a lemon drop?"
"A what?"
"A lemon drop. They're a kind of Muggle sweet I'm rather fond of"
"No, thank you," said Professor Romero coldly, as though she didn't think this was the moment for lemon drops. "As I say, even if You-Know-Who has gone -"
"My dear Professor, surely a sensible person like yourself can call him
by his name? All this 'You- Know-Who' nonsense -- for eleven years I
have been trying to persuade people to call him by his proper name: Salvador." Professor Romero flinched, but Dominguez, who was unsticking two lemon drops, seemed not to notice. "It all gets so
confusing if we keep saying 'You-Know-Who.' I have never seen any reason to be frightened of saying Salvador's name.
"I know you haven 't, said Professor Romero, sounding half exasperated, half admiring. "But you're different. Everyone knows you're the only one You-Know- oh, all right, Salvador, was frightened of."
"You flatter me," said Dominguez calmly. "Salvador had powers I will never have."
"Only because you're too -- well -- noble to use them."
"It's lucky it's dark. I haven't blushed so much since Madam Pomfrey told me she liked my new earmuffs."
Professor Romero shot a sharp look at Dominguez and said, "The owls are nothing next to the rumors that are flying around. You know what everyone's saying? About why he's disappeared? About what finally stopped him?"
It seemed that Professor Romero had reached the point she was most anxious to discuss, the real reason she had been waiting on a cold, hard wall all day, for neither as a cat nor as a woman had she fixed Dominguez with such a piercing stare as she did now. It was plain that whatever "everyone" was saying, she was not going to believe it until
8
Dominguez told her it was true. Dominguez, however, was choosing another lemon drop and did not answer.
"What they're saying," she pressed on, "is that last night Salvador turned up in Godric's Hollow. He went to find the Gonzaless. The rumor is that Sofia and Ricardo Gonzales are -- are -- that they're -- dead. "
Dominguez bowed his head. Professor Romero gasped.
"Sofia and Ricardo... I can't believe it... I didn't want to believe it... Oh, Alfonso..."
Dominguez reached out and patted her on the shoulder. "I know... I know..." he said heavily.
Professor Romero's voice trembled as she went on. "That's not all. They're saying he tried to kill the Gonzales's son, Roberto. But -- he couldn't. He couldn't kill that little boy. No one knows why, or how, but they're saying that when he couldn't kill Roberto Gonzales, Salvador's power somehow broke -- and that's why he's gone.
Dominguez nodded glumly.
"It's -- it's true?" faltered Professor Romero. "After all he's done... all the people he's killed... he couldn't kill a little boy?
It's just astounding... of all the things to stop him... but how in the name of heaven did Roberto survive?"
"We can only guess," said Dominguez. "We may never know."
Professor Romero pulled out a lace handkerchief and dabbed at her eyes beneath her spectacles. Dominguez gave a great sniff as he took a golden watch from his pocket and examined it. It was a very odd watch.
It had twelve hands but no numbers; instead, little planets were moving around the edge. It must have made sense to Dominguez, though, because he put it back in his pocket and said, "Villalba's late. I suppose it was
he who told you I'd be here, by the way?"
"Yes," said Professor Romero. "And I don't suppose you're going to tell me why you're here, of all places?"
"I've come to bring Roberto to his aunt and uncle. They're the only family he has left now."
9
"You don't mean -- you can't mean the people who live here?" cried Professor Romero, jumping to her feet and pointing at number four. "Dominguez -- you can't. I've been watching them all day. You couldn't find two people who are less like us. And they've got this son -- I saw him kicking his mother all the way up the street, screaming for sweets. Roberto Gonzales come and live here!"
"It's the best place for him," said Dominguez firmly. "His aunt and uncle will be able to explain everything to him when he's older. I've written them a letter."
"A letter?" repeated Professor Romero faintly, sitting back down on the wall. "Really, Dominguez, you think you can explain all this in a letter? These people will never understand him! He'll be famous -- a legend -- I wouldn't be surprised if today was known as Roberto Gonzales day in the future -- there will be books written about Roberto -- every child
in our world will know his name!"
"Exactly," said Dominguez, looking very seriously over the top of his half-moon glasses. "It would be enough to turn any boy's head. Famous before he can walk and talk! Famous for something he won't even remember! CarA you see how much better off he'll be, growing up away from all that until he's ready to take it?"
Professor Romero opened her mouth, changed her mind, swallowed, and then said, "Yes -- yes, you're right, of course. But how is the boy
getting here, Dominguez?" She eyed his cloak suddenly as though she thought he might be hiding Roberto underneath it.
"Villalba's bringing him."
"You think it -- wise -- to trust Villalba with something as important as this?"
I would trust Villalba with my life," said Dominguez.
"I'm not saying his heart isn't in the right place," said Professor Romero grudgingly, "but you can't pretend he's not careless. He does tend to -- what was that?"
A low rumbling sound had broken the silence around them. It grew steadily louder as they looked up and down the street for some sign of a
10
headlight; it swelled to a roar as they both looked up at the sky -- and a huge motorcycle fell out of the air and landed on the road in front of them.
If the motorcycle was huge, it was nothing to the man sitting astride
it. He was almost twice as tall as a normal man and at least five times as wide. He looked simply too big to be allowed, and so wild - long tangles of bushy black hair and beard hid most of his face, he had hands the size of trash can lids, and his feet in their leather boots were
like baby dolphins. In his vast, muscular arms he was holding a bundle of blankets.
"Villalba," said Dominguez, sounding relieved. "At last. And where did you get that motorcycle?"
"Borrowed it, Professor Dominguez, sit," said the giant, climbing carefully off the motorcycle as he spoke. "Young Gustavo Martinez lent it to me. I've got him, sir."
"No problems, were there?"
"No, sir -- house was almost destroyed, but I got him out all right before the Muggles started swarmin' around. He fell asleep as we was flyin' over Bristol."
Dominguez and Professor Romero bent forward over the bundle of blankets. Inside, just visible, was a baby boy, fast asleep. Under a
tuft of jet-black hair over his forehead they could see a curiously
shaped cut, like a bolt of lightning.
"Is that where -?" whispered Professor Romero. "Yes," said Dominguez. "He'll have that scar forever." "Couldn't you do something about it, Dominguez?"
"Even if I could, I wouldn't. Scars can come in handy. I have one myself above my left knee that is a perfect map of the London Underground. Well -- give him here, Villalba -- we'd better get this over with."
Dominguez took Roberto in his arms and turned toward the Carrizos' house. "Could I -- could I say good-bye to him, sir?" asked Villalba. He bent his
11
great, shaggy head over Roberto and gave him what must have been a very scratchy, whiskery kiss. Then, suddenly, Villalba let out a howl like a wounded dog.
"Shhh!" hissed Professor Romero, "you'll wake the Muggles!"
"S-s-sorry," sobbed Villalba, taking out a large, spotted handkerchief and burying his face in it. "But I c-c-can't stand it -- Sofia an' Ricardo dead
-- an' poor little Roberto off ter live with Muggles -"
"Yes, yes, it's all very sad, but get a grip on yourself, Villalba, or
we'll be found," Professor Romero whispered, patting Villalba gingerly on the arm as Dominguez stepped over the low garden wall and walked to the front door. He laid Roberto gently on the doorstep, took a letter out
of his cloak, tucked it inside Roberto's blankets, and then came back to
the other two. For a full minute the three of them stood and looked at
the little bundle; Villalba's shoulders shook, Professor Romero
blinked furiously, and the twinkling light that usually shone from Dominguez's eyes seemed to have gone out.
"Well," said Dominguez finally, "that's that. We've no business staying here. We may as well go and join the celebrations."
"Yeah," said Villalba in a very muffled voice, "I'll be takin' Gustavo his bike back. G'night, Professor Romero -- Professor Dominguez, sir."
Wiping his streaming eyes on his jacket sleeve, Villalba swung himself onto the motorcycle and kicked the engine into life; with a roar it rose into the air and off into the night.
"I shall see you soon, I expect, Professor Romero," said Dominguez, nodding to her. Professor Romero blew her nose in reply.
Dominguez turned and walked back down the street. On the corner he stopped and took out the silver Put-Outer. He clicked it once, and
twelve balls of light sped back to their street lamps so that Privet
Drive glowed suddenly orange and he could make out a tabby cat slinking around the corner at the other end of the street. He could just see the bundle of blankets on the step of number four.
"Good luck, Roberto," he murmured. He turned on his heel and with a swish of his cloak, he was gone.
12
A breeze ruffled the neat hedges of Privet Drive, which lay silent and tidy under the inky sky, the very last place you would expect astonishing things to happen. Roberto Gonzales rolled over inside his blankets without waking up. One small hand closed on the letter beside him and he slept on, not knowing he was special, not knowing he was famous, not knowing he would be woken in a few hours' time by Mrs. Carrizo's scream as she opened the front door to put out the milk bottles, nor that he would spend the next few weeks being prodded and pinched by his cousin Leonardo... He couldn't know that at this very moment, people meeting in secret all over the country were holding up their glasses and saying in hushed voices: "To Roberto Gonzales -- the boy who lived!"
CHAPTER TWO
THE VANISHING GLASS
Nearly ten years had passed since the Carrizos had woken up to find
their nephew on the front step, but Privet Drive had hardly changed at
all. The sun rose on the same tidy front gardens and lit up the brass number four on the Carrizos' front door; it crept into their living
room, which was almost exactly the same as it had been on the night when Mr. Carrizo had seen that fateful news report about the owls. Only the photographs on the mantelpiece really showed how much time had passed. Ten years ago, there had been lots of pictures of what looked like a
large pink beach ball wearing different-colored bonnets -- but Leonardo Carrizo was no longer a baby, and now the photographs showed a large blond boy riding his first bicycle, on a carousel at the fair, playing a computer game with his father, being hugged and kissed by his mother. The room held no sign at all that another boy lived in the house, too.
Yet Roberto Gonzales was still there, asleep at the moment, but not for
long. His Aunt Beatriz was awake and it was her shrill voice that made the first noise of the day.
"Up! Get up! Now!"
Roberto woke with a start. His aunt rapped on the door again.
"Up!" she screeched. Roberto heard her walking toward the kitchen and then the sound of the frying pan being put on the stove. He rolled onto his
back and tried to remember the dream he had been having. It had been a
13
good one. There had been a flying motorcycle in it. He had a funny feeling he'd had the same dream before.
His aunt was back outside the door.
"Are you up yet?" she demanded.
"Nearly," said Roberto.
"Well, get a move on, I want you to look after the bacon. And don't you dare let it burn, I want everything perfect on Duddy's birthday."
Roberto groaned.
"What did you say?" his aunt snapped through the door. "Nothing, nothing..."
Leonardo's birthday -- how could he have forgotten? Roberto got slowly out of bed and started looking for socks. He found a pair under his bed and, after pulling a spider off one of them, put them on. Roberto was used to spiders, because the cupboard under the stairs was full of them, and that was where he slept.
When he was dressed he went down the hall into the kitchen. The table was almost hidden beneath all Leonardo's birthday presents. It looked as though Leonardo had gotten the new computer he wanted, not to mention the second television and the racing bike. Exactly why Leonardo wanted a racing bike was a mystery to Roberto, as Leonardo was very fat and hated exercise -- unless of course it involved punching somebody. Leonardo's favorite punching bag was Roberto, but he couldn't often catch him. Roberto didn't look it, but he was very fast.
Perhaps it had something to do with living in a dark cupboard, but Roberto had always been small and skinny for his age. He looked even smaller and skinnier than he really was because all he had to wear were old clothes
of Leonardo's, and Leonardo was about four times bigger than he was. Roberto had a thin face, knobbly knees, black hair, and bright green eyes. He
wore round glasses held together with a lot of Scotch tape because of
all the times Leonardo had punched him on the nose. The only thing Roberto liked about his own appearance was a very thin scar on his forehead that was shaped like a bolt of lightning. He had had it as long as he could remember, and the first question he could ever remember asking his Aunt
14
Beatriz was how he had gotten it.
"In the car crash when your parents died," she had said. "And don't ask questions."
Don't ask questions -- that was the first rule for a quiet life with the Carrizos.
Uncle Francisco entered the kitchen as Roberto was turning over the bacon. "Comb your hair!" he barked, by way of a morning greeting.
About once a week, Uncle Francisco looked over the top of his newspaper and shouted that Roberto needed a haircut. Roberto must have had more haircuts than the rest of the boys in his class put
together, but it made no difference, his hair simply grew that way -- all over the place.
Roberto was frying eggs by the time Leonardo arrived in the kitchen with his mother. Leonardo looked a lot like Uncle Francisco. He had a large pink face, not much neck, small, watery blue eyes, and thick blond hair that lay smoothly on his thick, fat head. Aunt Beatriz often said that Leonardo looked like a baby angel -- Roberto often said that Leonardo looked like a
pig in a wig.
Roberto put the plates of egg and bacon on the table, which was difficult
as there wasn't much room. Leonardo, meanwhile, was counting his presents. His face fell.
"Thirty-six," he said, looking up at his mother and father. "That's two less than last year."
"Darling, you haven't counted Auntie Marge's present, see, it's here under this big one from Mommy and Daddy."
"All right, thirty-seven then," said Leonardo, going red in the face.
Roberto, who could see a huge Leonardo tantrum coming on, began wolfing down his bacon as fast as possible in case Leonardo turned the table over.
Aunt Beatriz obviously scented danger, too, because she said quickly, "And we'll buy you another two presents while we're out today. How's that, popkin? Two more presents. Is that all right''
15
Leonardo thought for a moment. It looked like hard work. Finally he said slowly, "So I'll have thirty ... thirty..."
"Thirty-nine, sweetums," said Aunt Beatriz.
"Oh." Leonardo sat down heavily and grabbed the nearest parcel. "All right then."
Uncle Francisco chuckled. "Little tyke wants his money's worth, just like his father. 'Atta boy, Leonardo!" He ruffled Leonardo's hair.
At that moment the telephone rang and Aunt Beatriz went to answer it while Roberto and Uncle Francisco watched Leonardo unwrap the racing bike, a video camera, a remote control airplane, sixteen new computer games, and a VCR. He was ripping the paper off a gold wristwatch when Aunt Beatriz came back from the telephone looking both angry and worried.
"Bad news, Francisco," she said. "Mrs. Figg's broken her leg. She can't take him." She jerked her head in Roberto's direction.
Leonardo's mouth fell open in horror, but Roberto's heart gave a leap. Every year on Leonardo's birthday, his parents took him and a friend out for the day, to adventure parks, hamburger restaurants, or the movies. Every year, Roberto was left behind with Mrs. Figg, a mad old lady who lived two streets away. Roberto hated it there. The whole house smelled of cabbage and Mrs. Figg made him look at photographs of all the cats she'd ever owned.
"Now what?" said Aunt Beatriz, looking furiously at Roberto as though he'd planned this. Roberto knew he ought to feel sorry that Mrs. Figg had
broken her leg, but it wasn't easy when he reminded himself it would be
a whole year before he had to look at Tibbles, Snowy, Mr. Paws, and Tufty again.
"We could phone Marge," Uncle Francisco suggested. "Don't be silly, Francisco, she hates the boy."
The Carrizos often spoke about Roberto like this, as though he wasn't there -- or rather, as though he was something very nasty that couldn't understand them, like a slug.
16
"What about what's-her-name, your friend -- Yvonne?" "On vacation in Majorca," snapped Aunt Beatriz.
"You could just leave me here," Roberto put in hopefully (he'd be able to watch what he wanted on television for a change and maybe even have a go on Leonardo's computer).
Aunt Beatriz looked as though she'd just swallowed a lemon.
"And come back and find the house in ruins?" she snarled.
"I won't blow up the house," said Roberto, but they weren't listening.
"I suppose we could take him to the zoo," said Aunt Beatriz slowly, "... and leave him in the car...."
"That car's new, he's not sitting in it alone...."
Leonardo began to cry loudly. In fact, he wasn't really crying -- it had been years since he'd really cried -- but he knew that if he screwed up his face and wailed, his mother would give him anything he wanted.
"Dinky Duddydums, don't cry, Mummy won't let him spoil your special day!" she cried, flinging her arms around him.
"I... don't... want... him... t-t-to come!" Leonardo yelled between huge, pretend sobs. "He always sp- spoils everything!" He shot Roberto a nasty grin through the gap in his mother's arms.
Just then, the doorbell rang -- "Oh, good Lord, they're here!" said Aunt Beatriz frantically -- and a moment later, Leonardo's best friend, Piers Polkiss, walked in with his mother. Piers was a scrawny boy with a face like a rat. He was usually the one who held people's arms behind their backs while Leonardo hit them. Leonardo stopped pretending to cry at once.
Half an hour later, Roberto, who couldn't believe his luck, was sitting in the back of the Carrizos' car with Piers and Leonardo, on the way to the zoo for the first time in his life. His aunt and uncle hadn't been able to think of anything else to do with him, but before they'd left, Uncle Francisco had taken Roberto aside.
"I'm warning you," he had said, putting his large purple face right up
17
close to Roberto's, "I'm warning you now, boy -- any funny business, anything at all -- and you'll be in that cupboard from now until Christmas."
"I'm not going to do anything," said Roberto, "honestly.. But Uncle Francisco didn't believe him. No one ever did.
The problem was, strange things often happened around Roberto and it was just no good telling the Carrizos he didn't make them happen.
Once, Aunt Beatriz, tired of Roberto coming back from the barbers looking as though he hadn't been at all, had taken a pair of kitchen scissors
and cut his hair so short he was almost bald except for his bangs, which she left "to hide that horrible scar." Leonardo had laughed himself silly
at Roberto, who spent a sleepless night imagining school the next day, where he was already laughed at for his baggy clothes and taped glasses. Next morning, however, he had gotten up to find his hair exactly as it
had been before Aunt Beatriz had sheared it off He had been given a week in his cupboard for this, even though he had tried to explain that he couldn't explain how it had grown back so quickly.
Another time, Aunt Beatriz had been trying to force him into a revolting old sweater of Leonardo's (brown with orange puff balls) -- The harder she tried to pull it over his head, the smaller it seemed to become, until finally it might have fitted a hand puppet, but certainly wouldn't fit Roberto. Aunt Beatriz had decided it must have shrunk in the wash and, to his great relief, Roberto wasn't punished.
On the other hand, he'd gotten into terrible trouble for being found on the roof of the school kitchens. Leonardo's gang had been chasing him as usual when, as much to Roberto's surprise as anyone else's, there he was sitting on the chimney. The Carrizos had received a very angry letter from Roberto's headmistress telling them Roberto had been climbing school buildings. But all he'd tried to do (as he shouted at Uncle Francisco through the locked door of his cupboard) was jump behind the big trash cans outside the kitchen doors. Roberto supposed that the wind must have caught him in mid- jump.
But today, nothing was going to go wrong. It was even worth being with Leonardo and Piers to be spending the day somewhere that wasn't school, his cupboard, or Mrs. Figg's cabbage-smelling living room.
18
While he drove, Uncle Francisco complained to Aunt Beatriz. He liked to complain about things: people at work, Roberto, the council, Roberto, the bank, and Roberto were just a few of his favorite subjects. This morning, it was motorcycles.
"... roaring along like maniacs, the young hoodlums," he said, as a motorcycle overtook them.
I had a dream about a motorcycle," said Roberto, remembering suddenly. "It was flying."
Uncle Francisco nearly crashed into the car in front. He turned right around in his seat and yelled at Roberto, his face like a gigantic beet with a mustache: "MOTORCYCLES DON'T FLY!"
Leonardo and Piers sniggered.
I know they don't," said Roberto. "It was only a dream."
But he wished he hadn't said anything. If there was one thing the Carrizos hated even more than his asking questions, it was his talking about anything acting in a way it shouldn't, no matter if it was in a dream or even a cartoon -- they seemed to think he might get dangerous ideas.
It was a very sunny Saturday and the zoo was crowded with families. The Carrizos bought Leonardo and Piers large chocolate ice creams at the entrance and then, because the smiling lady in the van had asked Roberto what he wanted before they could hurry him away, they bought him a cheap lemon ice pop. It wasn't bad, either, Roberto thought, licking it as they watched a gorilla scratching its head who looked remarkably like Leonardo, except that it wasn't blond.
Roberto had the best morning he'd had in a long time. He was careful to walk a little way apart from the Carrizos so that Leonardo and Piers, who were starting to get bored with the animals by lunchtime, wouldn't fall back on their favorite hobby of hitting him. They ate in the zoo restaurant, and when Leonardo had a tantrum because his knickerbocker glory didn't have enough ice cream on top, Uncle Francisco bought him another one and Roberto was allowed to finish the first.
Roberto felt, afterward, that he should have known it was all too good to last.
19
After lunch they went to the reptile house. It was cool and dark in
there, with lit windows all along the walls. Behind the glass, all sorts
of lizards and snakes were crawling and slithering over bits of wood and stone. Leonardo and Piers wanted to see huge, poisonous cobras and thick, man-crushing pythons. Leonardo quickly found the largest snake in the place. It could have wrapped its body twice around Uncle Francisco's car and crushed it into a trash can -- but at the moment it didn't look in
the mood. In fact, it was fast asleep.
Leonardo stood with his nose pressed against the glass, staring at the glistening brown coils.
"Make it move," he whined at his father. Uncle Francisco tapped on the glass, but the snake didn't budge.
"Do it again," Leonardo ordered. Uncle Francisco rapped the glass smartly with his knuckles, but the snake just snoozed on.
"This is boring," Leonardo moaned. He shuffled away.
Roberto moved in front of the tank and looked intently at the snake. He wouldn't have been surprised if it had died of boredom itself -- no company except stupid people drumming their fingers on the glass trying to disturb it all day long. It was worse than having a cupboard as a bedroom, where the only visitor was Aunt Beatriz hammering on the door to wake you up; at least he got to visit the rest of the house.
The snake suddenly opened its beady eyes. Slowly, very slowly, it raised its head until its eyes were on a level with Roberto's.
It winked.
Roberto stared. Then he looked quickly around to see if anyone was watching. They weren't. He looked back at the snake and winked, too.
The snake jerked its head toward Uncle Francisco and Leonardo, then raised its eyes to the ceiling. It gave Roberto a look that said quite plainly:
"I get that all the time.
"I know," Roberto murmured through the glass, though he wasn't sure the snake could hear him. "It must be really annoying."
20
The snake nodded vigorously.
"Where do you come from, anyway?" Roberto asked.
The snake jabbed its tail at a little sign next to the glass. Roberto peered at it.
Boa Constrictor, Brazil. "Was it nice there?"
The boa constrictor jabbed its tail at the sign again and Roberto read on: This specimen was bred in the zoo. "Oh, I see -- so you've never been to Brazil?"
As the snake shook its head, a deafening shout behind Roberto made both of them jump.
"LEONARDO! MR. CARRIZO! COME AND LOOK AT THIS SNAKE! YOU WON'T BELIEVE
WHAT IT'S DOING!"
Leonardo came waddling toward them as fast as he could.
"Out of the way, you," he said, punching Roberto in the ribs. Caught by surprise, Roberto fell hard on the concrete floor. What came next happened so fast no one saw how it happened -- one second, Piers and Leonardo were leaning right up close to the glass, the next, they had leapt back with howls of horror.
Roberto sat up and gasped; the glass front of the boa constrictor's tank had vanished. The great snake was uncoiling itself rapidly, slithering out onto the floor. People throughout the reptile house screamed and started running for the exits.
As the snake slid swiftly past him, Roberto could have sworn a low, hissing voice said, "Brazil, here I come.... Thanksss, amigo."
The keeper of the reptile house was in shock.

21
The zoo director himself made Aunt Beatriz a cup of strong, sweet tea while he apologized over and over again. Piers and Leonardo could only gibber. As far as Roberto had seen, the snake hadn't done anything except snap playfully at their heels as it passed, but by the time they were
all back in Uncle Francisco's car, Leonardo was telling them how it had nearly bitten off his leg, while Piers was swearing it had tried to squeeze him to death. But worst of all, for Roberto at least, was Piers calming down enough to say, "Roberto was talking to it, weren't you, Roberto?"
Uncle Francisco waited until Piers was safely out of the house before starting on Roberto. He was so angry he could hardly speak. He managed to say, "Go -- cupboard -- stay -- no meals," before he collapsed into a chair, and Aunt Beatriz had to run and get him a large brandy.
Roberto lay in his dark cupboard much later, wishing he had a watch. He didn't know what time it was and he couldn't be sure the Carrizos were asleep yet. Until they were, he couldn't risk sneaking to the kitchen
for some food.
He'd lived with the Carrizos almost ten years, ten miserable years, as
long as he could remember, ever since he'd been a baby and his parents had died in that car crash. He couldn't remember being in the car when
his parents had died. Sometimes, when he strained his memory during long hours in his cupboard, he came up with a strange vision: a blinding
flash of green light and a burn- ing pain on his forehead. This, he supposed, was the crash, though he couldn't imagine where all the green light came from. He couldn't remember his parents at all. His aunt and uncle never spoke about them, and of course he was forbidden to ask questions. There were no photographs of them in the house.
When he had been younger, Roberto had dreamed and dreamed of some unknown relation coming to take him away, but it had never happened; the
Carrizos were his only family. Yet sometimes he thought (or maybe hoped) that strangers in the street seemed to know him. Very strange strangers
they were, too. A tiny man in a violet top hat had bowed to him once while out shopping with Aunt Beatriz and Leonardo. After asking Roberto furiously if he knew the man, Aunt Beatriz had rushed them out of the shop without buying anything. A wild-looking old woman dressed all in green had waved merrily at him once on a bus. A bald man in a very long purple coat had actually shaken his hand in the street the other day and then walked away without a word. The weirdest thing about all these people was the way they seemed to vanish the second Roberto tried to get a
22
closer look.
At school, Roberto had no one. Everybody knew that Leonardo's gang hated that odd Roberto Gonzales in his baggy old clothes and broken glasses, and nobody liked to disagree with Leonardo's gang.
CHAPTER THREE
THE LETTERS FROM NO ONE
The escape of the Brazilian boa constrictor earned Roberto his
longest-ever punishment. By the time he was allowed out of his cupboard again, the summer holidays had started and Leonardo had already broken his new video camera, crashed his remote control airplane, and, first time
out on his racing bike, knocked down old Mrs. Figg as she crossed Privet Drive on her crutches.
Roberto was glad school was over, but there was no escaping Leonardo's gang, who visited the house every single day. Piers, Dennis, Malcolm, and Gordon were all big and stupid, but as Leonardo was the biggest and stupidest of the lot, he was the leader. The rest of them were all quite happy to join in Leonardo's favorite sport: Roberto Hunting.
This was why Roberto spent as much time as possible out of the house, wandering around and thinking about the end of the holidays, where he could see a tiny ray of hope. When September came he would be going off to secondary school and, for the first time in his life, he wouldn't be
with Leonardo. Leonardo had been accepted at Uncle Francisco's old private school, Smeltings. Piers Polkiss was going there too. Roberto, on the
other hand, was going to Stonewall High, the local public school. Leonardo thought this was very funny.
"They stuff people's heads down the toilet the first day at Stonewall," he told Roberto. "Want to come upstairs and practice?"
"No, thanks," said Roberto. "The poor toilet's never had anything as horrible as your head down it -- it might be sick." Then he ran, before Leonardo could work out what he'd said.
One day in July, Aunt Beatriz took Leonardo to London to buy his Smeltings uniform, leaving Roberto at Mrs. Figg's. Mrs. Figg wasn 't as bad as
usual. It turned out she'd broken her leg tripping over one of her cats,
23
and she didn't seem quite as fond of them as before. She let Roberto watch television and gave him a bit of chocolate cake that tasted as though she'd had it for several years.
That evening, Leonardo paraded around the living room for the family in his brand-new uniform. Smeltings' boys wore maroon tailcoats, orange knickerbockers, and flat straw hats called boaters. They also carried knobbly sticks, used for hitting each other while the teachers weren't looking. This was supposed to be good training for later life.
As he looked at Leonardo in his new knickerbockers, Uncle Francisco said gruffly that it was the proudest moment of his life. Aunt Beatriz burst
into tears and said she couldn't believe it was her Ickle Leonardokins, he looked so handsome and grown-up. Roberto didn't trust himself to speak. He thought two of his ribs might already have cracked from trying not to laugh.
There was a horrible smell in the kitchen the next morning when Roberto went in for breakfast. It seemed to be coming from a large metal tub in the sink. He went to have a look. The tub was full of what looked like dirty rags swimming in gray water.
"What's this?" he asked Aunt Beatriz. Her lips tightened as they always did if he dared to ask a question.
"Your new school uniform," she said.
Roberto looked in the bowl again.
"Oh," he said, "I didn't realize it had to be so wet."

finished."
Roberto seriously doubted this, but thought it best not to argue. He sat down at the table and tried not to think about how he was going to look on his first day at Stonewall High -- like he was wearing bits of old elephant skin, probably.
Leonardo and Uncle Francisco came in, both with wrinkled noses because of the smell from Roberto's new uniform. Uncle Francisco opened his newspaper as usual and Leonardo banged his Smelting stick, which he carried everywhere,

Roberto picked it up and stared at it, his heart twanging like a giant elastic band. No one, ever, in his whole life, had written to him. Who would? He had no friends, no other relatives -- he didn't belong to the library, so he'd never even got rude notes asking for books back. The envelope was thick and heavy, made of yellowish parchment, and the address was written in emerald-green ink. There was no stamp.

The Carrizos' house had four bedrooms: one for Uncle Francisco and Aunt Beatriz, one for visitors (usually Uncle Francisco's sister, Marge), one where Leonardo slept, and one where Leonardo kept all the toys and things that wouldn't fit into his first bedroom. It only took Roberto one trip
upstairs to move everything he owned from the cupboard to this room. He sat down on the bed and stared around him. Nearly everything in here was broken. The month-old video camera was lying on top of a small, working tank Leonardo had once driven over the next door neighbor's dog; in the corner was Leonardo's first-ever television set, which he'd put his foot through when his favorite program had been canceled; there was a large birdcage, which had once held a parrot that Leonardo had swapped at school for a real air rifle, which was up on a shelf with the end all bent
because Leonardo had sat on it. Other shelves were full of books. They were the only things in the room that looked as though they'd never been touched.
From downstairs came the sound of Leonardo bawling at his mother, I don't want him in there... I need that room... make him get out...."
Roberto sighed and stretched out on the bed. Yesterday he'd have given anything to be up here. Today he'd rather be back in his cupboard with that letter than up here without it.
Next morning at breakfast, everyone was rather quiet. Leonardo was in shock. He'd screamed, whacked his father with his Smelting stick, been sick on purpose, kicked his mother, and thrown his tortoise through the greenhouse roof, and he still didn't have his room back. Roberto was

thinking about this time yesterday and bitterly wishing he'd opened the letter in the hall. Uncle Francisco and Aunt Beatriz kept looking at each other darkly.
When the mail arrived, Uncle Francisco, who seemed to be trying to be nice to Roberto, made Leonardo go and get it. They heard him banging things with his Smelting stick all the way down the hall. Then he shouted, "There's another one! 'Mr. H. Gonzales, The Smallest Bedroom, 4 Privet Drive --'"
With a strangled cry, Uncle Francisco leapt from his seat and ran down the hall, Roberto right behind him. Uncle Francisco had to wrestle Leonardo to the ground to get the letter from him, which was made difficult by the fact
that Roberto had grabbed Uncle Francisco around the neck from behind. After a minute of confused fighting, in which everyone got hit a lot by the Smelting stick, Uncle Francisco straightened up, gasping for breath, with Roberto's letter clutched in his hand.

Roberto walked round and round his new room. Someone knew he had moved out of his cupboard and they seemed to know he hadn't received his first

He was going to wait for the postman on the corner of Privet Drive and get the letters for number four first. His heart hammered as he crept across the dark hall toward the front door --

He looked so dangerous with half his mustache missing that no one dared argue. Ten minutes later they had wrenched their way through the boarded-up doors and were in the car, speeding toward the highway. Leonardo was sniffling in the back seat; his father had hit him round the head for holding them up while he tried to pack his television, VCR, and computer in his sports bag.
They drove. And they drove. Even Aunt Beatriz didn't dare ask where they were going. Every now and then Uncle Francisco would take a sharp turn and drive in the opposite direction for a while. "Shake'em off... shake 'em

They didn't stop to eat or drink all day. By nightfall Leonardo was howling. He'd never had such a bad day in his life. He was hungry, he'd missed five television programs he'd wanted to see, and he'd never gone so long without blowing up an alien on his computer.
Uncle Francisco stopped at last outside a gloomy-looking hotel on the outskirts of a big city. Leonardo and Roberto shared a room with twin beds and damp, musty sheets. Leonardo snored but Roberto stayed awake, sitting on the windowsill, staring down at the lights of passing cars and wondering....
They ate stale cornflakes and cold tinned tomatoes on toast for breakfast the next day. They had just finished when the owner of the hotel came over to their table.

He drove them into the middle of a forest, got out, looked around, shook his head, got back in the car, and off they went again. "Found the perfect place!" he said. "Come on! Everyone out!"
It was very cold outside the car. Uncle Francisco was pointing at what looked like a large rock way out at sea. Perched on top of the rock was the most miserable little shack you could imagine. One thing was certain, there was no television in there.
"Storm forecast for tonight!" said Uncle Francisco gleefully, clapping his hands together. "And this gentleman's kindly agreed to lend us his boat!"
A toothless old man came ambling up to them, pointing, with a rather wicked grin, at an old rowboat bobbing in the iron-gray water below them.
"I've already got us some rations," said Uncle Francisco, "so all aboard!"
It was freezing in the boat. Icy sea spray and rain crept down their necks and a chilly wind whipped their faces. After what seemed like hours they reached the rock, where Uncle Francisco, slipping and sliding, led the way to the broken-down house.
The inside was horrible; it smelled strongly of seaweed, the wind whistled through the gaps in the wooden walls, and the fireplace was damp and empty. There were only two rooms.
Uncle Francisco's rations turned out to be a bag of chips each and four bananas. He tried to start a fire but the empty chip bags just smoked and shriveled up.
"Could do with some of those letters now, eh?" he said cheerfully.
He was in a very good mood. Obviously he thought nobody stood a chance of reaching them here in a storm to deliver mail. Roberto privately
agreed, though the thought didn't cheer him up at all.
As night fell, the promised storm blew up around them. Spray from the high waves splattered the walls of the hut and a fierce wind rattled the filthy windows. Aunt Beatriz found a few moldy blankets in the second
33
room and made up a bed for Leonardo on the moth-eaten sofa. She and Uncle Francisco went off to the lumpy bed next door, and Roberto was left to find
the softest bit of floor he could and to curl up under the thinnest,
most ragged blanket.
The storm raged more and more ferociously as the night went on. Roberto couldn't sleep. He shivered and turned over, trying to get comfortable, his stomach rumbling with hunger. Leonardo's snores were drowned by the low rolls of thunder that started near midnight. The lighted dial of Leonardo's watch, which was dangling over the edge of the sofa on his fat wrist, told Roberto he'd be eleven in ten minutes' time. He lay and
watched his birthday tick nearer, wondering if the Carrizos would remember at all, wondering where the letter writer was now.
Five minutes to go. Roberto heard something creak outside. He hoped the roof wasn't going to fall in, although he might be warmer if it did.
Four minutes to go. Maybe the house in Privet Drive would be so full of letters when they got back that he'd be able to steal one somehow.
Three minutes to go. Was that the sea, slapping hard on the rock like that? And (two minutes to go) what was that funny crunching noise? Was the rock crumbling into the sea?
One minute to go and he'd be eleven. Thirty seconds... twenty ... ten... nine -- maybe he'd wake Leonardo up, just to annoy him -- three... two... one...
BOOM.
The whole shack shivered and Roberto sat bolt upright, staring at the door. Someone was outside, knocking to come in.
CHAPTER FOUR
THE KEEPER OF THE KEYS
BOOM. They knocked again. Leonardo jerked awake. "Where's the cannon?" he said stupidly.
There was a crash behind them and Uncle Francisco came skidding into the room. He was holding a rifle in his hands -- now they knew what had been in the long, thin package he had brought with them.
34
"Who's there?" he shouted. "I warn you -- I'm armed!"
There was a pause. Then --
SMASH!
The door was hit with such force that it swung clean off its hinges and with a deafening crash landed flat on the floor.
A giant of a man was standing in the doorway. His face was almost completely hidden by a long, shaggy mane of hair and a wild, tangled beard, but you could make out his eyes, glinting like black beetles under all the hair.
The giant squeezed his way into the hut, stooping so that his head just brushed the ceiling. He bent down, picked up the door, and fitted it easily back into its frame. The noise of the storm outside dropped a little. He turned to look at them all.
"Couldn't make us a cup o' tea, could yeh? It's not been an easy journey..."
He strode over to the sofa where Leonardo sat frozen with fear. "Budge up, yeh great lump," said the stranger.
Leonardo squeaked and ran to hide behind his mother, who was crouching, terrified, behind Uncle Francisco.
"An' here's Roberto!" said the giant.
Roberto looked up into the fierce, wild, shadowy face and saw that the beetle eyes were crinkled in a smile.
"Las' time I saw you, you was only a baby," said the giant. "Yeh look a lot like yet dad, but yeh've got yet mom's eyes."
Uncle Francisco made a funny rasping noise.
I demand that you leave at once, sit!" he said. "You are breaking and entering!"
35
"Ah, shut up, Carrizo, yeh great prune," said the giant; he reached over the back of the sofa, jerked the gun out of Uncle Francisco's hands, bent it into a knot as easily as if it had been made of rubber, and threw it into a corner of the room.
Uncle Francisco made another funny noise, like a mouse being trodden on.
"Anyway -- Roberto," said the giant, turning his back on the Carrizos, "a very happy birthday to yeh. Got summat fer yeh here -- I mighta sat on it at some point, but it'll taste all right."
From an inside pocket of his black overcoat he pulled a slightly squashed box. Roberto opened it with trembling fingers. Inside was a large, sticky chocolate cake with Happy Birthday Roberto written on it in green icing.
Roberto looked up at the giant. He meant to say thank you, but the words got lost on the way to his mouth, and what he said instead was, "Who are you?"
The giant chuckled.
"True, I haven't introduced meself. Matias Villalba, Keeper of Keys and Grounds at Hogwarts."
He held out an enormous hand and shook Roberto's whole arm.
"What about that tea then, eh?" he said, rubbing his hands together. "I'd not say no ter summat stronger if yeh've got it, mind."
His eyes fell on the empty grate with the shriveled chip bags in it and
he snorted. He bent down over the fireplace; they couldn't see what he was doing but when he drew back a second later, there was a roaring fire there. It filled the whole damp hut with flickering light and Roberto felt the warmth wash over him as though he'd sunk into a hot bath.
The giant sat back down on the sofa, which sagged under his weight, and began taking all sorts of things out of the pockets of his coat: a
copper kettle, a squashy package of sausages, a poker, a teapot, several chipped mugs, and a bottle of some amber liquid that he took a swig from before starting to make tea. Soon the hut was full of the sound and
smell of sizzling sausage. Nobody said a thing while the giant was working, but as he slid the first six fat, juicy, slightly burnt
36
sausages from the poker, Leonardo fidgeted a little. Uncle Francisco said sharply, "Don't touch anything he gives you, Leonardo."
The giant chuckled darkly.
"Yet great puddin' of a son don' need fattenin' anymore, Carrizo, don' worry."
He passed the sausages to Roberto, who was so hungry he had never tasted anything so wonderful, but he still couldn't take his eyes off the
giant. Finally, as nobody seemed about to explain anything, he said, "I'm sorry, but I still don't really know who you are."
The giant took a gulp of tea and wiped his mouth with the back of his hand.
"Call me Villalba," he said, "everyone does. An' like I told yeh, I'm Keeper of Keys at Hogwarts -- yeh'll know all about Hogwarts, o' course.
"Er -- no," said Roberto. Villalba looked shocked. "Sorry," Roberto said quickly.
"Sony?" barked Villalba, turning to stare at the Carrizos, who shrank back into the shadows. "It' s them as should be sorry! I knew yeh weren't gettin' yer letters but I never thought yeh wouldn't even know abou' Hogwarts, fer cryin' out loud! Did yeh never wonder where yet parents learned it all?"
"All what?" asked Roberto.
"ALL WHAT?" Villalba thundered. "Now wait jus' one second!"
He had leapt to his feet. In his anger he seemed to fill the whole hut. The Carrizos were cowering against the wall.
"Do you mean ter tell me," he growled at the Carrizos, "that this boy -- this boy! -- knows nothin' abou' -- about ANYTHING?"
Roberto thought this was going a bit far. He had been to school, after all, and his marks weren't bad.
37
"I know some things," he said. "I can, you know, do math and stuff." But Villalba simply waved his hand and said, "About our world, I mean. Your world. My world. Yer parents' world."
"What world?"
Villalba looked as if he was about to explode.
"CARRIZO!" he boomed.
Uncle Francisco, who had gone very pale, whispered something that sounded like "Mimblewimble." Villalba stared wildly at Roberto.
"But yeh must know about yet mom and dad," he said. "I mean, they're famous. You're famous."
"What? My -- my mom and dad weren't famous, were they?"
"Yeh don' know... yeh don' know..." Villalba ran his fingers through his hair, fixing Roberto with a bewildered stare.
"Yeh don' know what yeh are?" he said finally. Uncle Francisco suddenly found his voice.
"Stop!" he commanded. "Stop right there, sit! I forbid you to tell the boy anything!"
A braver man than Francisco Carrizo would have quailed under the furious look Villalba now gave him; when Villalba spoke, his every syllable trembled with rage.
"You never told him? Never told him what was in the letter Dominguez left fer him? I was there! I saw Dominguez leave it, Carrizo! An' you've kept it from him all these years?"
"Kept what from me?" said Roberto eagerly.
"STOP! I FORBID YOU!" yelled Uncle Francisco in panic. Aunt Beatriz gave a gasp of horror.
38
"Ah, go boil yet heads, both of yeh," said Villalba. "Roberto -- yet a wizard."
There was silence inside the hut. Only the sea and the whistling wind could be heard.
"-- a what?" gasped Roberto.
"A wizard, o' course," said Villalba, sitting back down on the sofa, which groaned and sank even lower, "an' a thumpin' good'un, I'd say, once yeh've been trained up a bit. With a mum an' dad like yours, what else would yeh be? An' I reckon it's abou' time yeh read yer letter."
Roberto stretched out his hand at last to take the yellowish envelope, addressed in emerald green to Mr. H. Gonzales, The Floor, Hut-on-the-Rock, The Sea. He pulled out the letter and read:
HOGWARTS SPAULAOL of WITCHCRAFT and WIZARDRY Headmaster: ALFONSO DOMINGUEZ
(Order of Merlin, First Class, Grand Sorc., Chf. Warlock, Supreme Mugwump, International Confed. of Wizards)
Dear Mr. Gonzales,
We are pleased to inform you that you have been accepted at Hogwarts School of Witchcraft and Wizardry. Please find enclosed a list of all necessary books and equipment.
Term begins on September 1. We await your owl by no later than July 31. Yours sincerely,
Elena Romero, Deputy Headmistress
Questions exploded inside Roberto's head like fireworks and he couldn't decide which to ask first. After a few minutes he stammered, "What does it mean, they await my owl?"
"Gallopin' Gorgons, that reminds me," said Villalba, clapping a hand to his forehead with enough force to knock over a cart horse, and from yet
39
another pocket inside his overcoat he pulled an owl -- a real, live, rather ruffled-looking owl -- a long quill, and a roll of parchment. With his tongue between his teeth he scribbled a note that Roberto could read upside down:
Dear Professor Dominguez,
Given Roberto his letter.
Taking him to buy his things tomorrow. Weather's horrible. Hope you're Well. Villalba
Villalba rolled up the note, gave it to the owl, which clamped it in its beak, went to the door, and threw the owl out into the storm. Then he came back and sat down as though this was as normal as talking on the telephone.
Roberto realized his mouth was open and closed it quickly.
"Where was I?" said Villalba, but at that moment, Uncle Francisco, still ashen-faced but looking very angry, moved into the firelight.

could you not be, my dratted sister being what she was? But at that moment, Villalba leapt from the sofa and drew a battered pink umbrella from inside his coat. Pointing this at Uncle Francisco like a sword, he said, "I'm warning you, Carrizo -I'm warning you -- one more word... "
In danger of being speared on the end of an umbrella by a bearded giant, Uncle Francisco's courage failed again; he flattened himself against the wall and fell silent.
"That's better," said Villalba, breathing heavily and sitting back down on the sofa, which this time sagged right down to the floor.

Villalba looked at Roberto with warmth and respect blazing in his eyes, but Roberto, instead of feeling pleased and proud, felt quite sure there had

To his surprise, Villalba chuckled.

Roberto looked into the fire. Now he came to think about it... every odd thing that had ever made his aunt and uncle furious with him had happened when he, Roberto, had been upset or angry... chased by Leonardo's gang, he had somehow found himself out of their reach... dreading going to school with that ridiculous haircut, he'd managed to make it grow back... and the very last time Leonardo had hit him, hadn't he got his

revenge, without even realizing he was doing it? Hadn't he set a boa constrictor on him?

CHAPTER FIVE DIAGON ALLEY
Roberto woke early the next morning. Although he could tell it was daylight, he kept his eyes shut tight.
"It was a dream, he told himself firmly. "I dreamed a giant called
Villalba came to tell me I was going to a school for wizards. When I open my eyes I'll be at home in my cupboard."
There was suddenly a loud tapping noise.
And there's Aunt Beatriz knocking on the door, Roberto thought, his heart sinking. But he still didn't open his eyes. It had been such a good
46
dream.
Tap. Tap. Tap.
"All right," Roberto mumbled, "I'm getting up."
He sat up and Villalba's heavy coat fell off him. The hut was full of sunlight, the storm was over, Villalba himself was asleep on the collapsed sofa, and there was an owl rapping its claw on the window, a newspaper held in its beak.
Roberto scrambled to his feet, so happy he felt as though a large balloon
was swelling inside him. He went straight to the window and jerked it
open. The owl swooped in and dropped the newspaper on top of Villalba, who didn't wake up. The owl then fluttered onto the floor and began to
attack Villalba's coat.
"Don't do that."
Roberto tried to wave the owl out of the way, but it snapped its beak fiercely at him and carried on savaging the coat.
"Villalba!" said Roberto loudly. "There's an owl "Pay him," Villalba grunted into the sofa. "What?"
"He wants payin' fer deliverin' the paper. Look in the pockets." Villalba's coat seemed to be made of nothing but pockets -- bunches of keys, slug pellets, balls of string, peppermint humbugs, teabags... finally, Roberto pulled out a handful of strange-looking coins.
"Give him five Knuts," said Villalba sleepily. "Knuts?"
"The little bronze ones."
Roberto counted out five little bronze coins, and the owl held out his leg so Roberto could put the money into a small leather pouch tied to it. Then he flew off through the open window.
47
Villalba yawned loudly, sat up, and stretched.
"Best be Off, Roberto, lots ter do today, gotta get up ter London an' buy all yer stuff fer school."
Roberto was turning over the wizard coins and looking at them. He had just thought of something that made him feel as though the happy balloon inside him had got a puncture.
"Um -- Villalba?"
"Mm?" said Villalba, who was pulling on his huge boots.
"I haven't got any money -- and you heard Uncle Francisco last night ... he won't pay for me to go and learn magic."
"Don't worry about that," said Villalba, standing up and scratching his head. "D'yeh think yer parents didn't leave yeh anything?"
"But if their house was destroyed --"
"They didn' keep their gold in the house, boy! Nah, first stop fer us is Gringotts. Wizards' bank. Have a sausage, they're not bad cold -- an' I wouldn' say no teh a bit o' yer birthday cake, neither."

"Just the one. Gringotts. Run by goblins."
Roberto dropped the bit of sausage he was holding. "Goblins?"
"Yeah -- so yeh'd be mad ter try an' rob it, I'll tell yeh that. Never
mess with goblins, Roberto. Gringotts is the safest place in the world fer anything yeh want ter keep safe -- 'cept maybe Hogwarts. As a matter o' fact, I gotta visit Gringotts anyway. Fer Dominguez. Hogwarts business." Villalba drew himself up proudly. "He usually gets me ter do important stuff fer him. Fetchin' you gettin' things from Gringotts -- knows he can trust me, see.
"Got everythin'? Come on, then."

Roberto followed Villalba out onto the rock. The sky was quite clear now and the sea gleamed in the sunlight. The boat Uncle Francisco had hired was still there, with a lot of water in the bottom after the storm.
"How did you get here?" Roberto asked, looking around for another boat. "Flew," said Villalba.
"Flew?"
"Yeah -- but we'll go back in this. Not s'pposed ter use magic now I've got yeh."
They settled down in the boat, Roberto still staring at Villalba, trying to imagine him flying.
"Seems a shame ter row, though," said Villalba, giving Roberto another of his sideways looks. "If I was ter -- er -- speed things up a bit, would yeh mind not mentionin' it at Hogwarts?"
"Of course not," said Roberto, eager to see more magic. Villalba pulled out the pink umbrella again, tapped it twice on the side of the boat, and they sped off toward land.
"Why would you be mad to try and rob Gringotts?" Roberto asked.
"Spells -- enchantments," said Villalba, unfolding his newspaper as he spoke. "They say there's dragons guardin' the highsecurity vaults. And then yeh gotta find yer way -- Gringotts is hundreds of miles under London, see. Deep under the Underground. Yeh'd die of hunger tryin' ter get out, even if yeh did manage ter get yer hands on summat."
Roberto sat and thought about this while Villalba read his newspaper, the Daily Prophet. Roberto had learned from Uncle Francisco that people liked to be left alone while they did this, but it was very difficult, he'd never
had so many questions in his life.
"Ministry o' Magic messin' things up as usual," Villalba muttered, turning the page.

"'Course," said Villalba. "They wanted Dominguez fer Minister, 0 '

course, but he'd never leave Hogwarts, so old Cornelius Fudge got the job. Bungler if ever there was one. So he pelts Dominguez with owls every morning, askin' fer advice."

"Well, their main job is to keep it from the Muggles that there's still witches an' wizards up an' down the country."

"Why? Blimey, Roberto, everyone'd be wantin' magic solutions to their problems. Nah, we're best left alone."
At this moment the boat bumped gently into the harbor wall. Villalba folded up his newspaper, and they clambered up the stone steps onto the street.
Passersby stared a lot at Villalba as they walked through the little town
to the station. Roberto couldn't blame them. Not only was Villalba twice as tall as anyone else, he kept pointing at perfectly ordinary things like parking meters and saying loudly, "See that, Roberto? Things these Muggles dream up, eh?"
"Villalba," said Roberto, panting a bit as he ran to keep up, "did you say there are dragons at Gringotts?"
"Well, so they say," said Villalba. "Crikey, I'd like a dragon." "You'd like one?"
"Wanted one ever since I was a kid -- here we go."
They had reached the station. There was a train to London in five minutes' time. Villalba, who didn't understand "Muggle money," as he called it, gave the bills to Roberto so he could buy their tickets.
People stared more than ever on the train. Villalba took up two seats and sat knitting what looked like a canary-yellow circus tent.
"Still got yer letter, Roberto?" he asked as he counted stitches. Roberto took the parchment envelope out of his pocket.
50
"Good," said Villalba. "There's a list there of everything yeh need."
Roberto unfolded a second piece of paper he hadn't noticed the night before, and read:
HOGWARTS SPAULAOL of WITCHCRAFT and WIZARDRY UNIFORM
First-year students will require:
1. Three sets of plain work robes (black)
2. One plain pointed hat (black) for day wear

BROOMSTICKS
"Can we buy all this in London?" Roberto wondered aloud. "If yeh know where to go," said Villalba.
Roberto had never been to London before. Although Villalba seemed to know where he was going, he was obviously not used to getting there in an ordinary way. He got stuck in the ticket barrier on the Underground, and complained loudly that the seats were too small and the trains too slow.
"I don't know how the Muggles manage without magic," he said as they climbed a broken-down escalator that led up to a bustling road lined with shops.
Villalba was so huge that he parted the crowd easily; all Roberto had to do was keep close behind him. They passed book shops and music stores, hamburger restaurants and cinemas, but nowhere that looked as if it could sell you a magic wand. This was just an ordinary street full of ordinary people. Could there really be piles of wizard gold buried miles beneath them? Were there really shops that sold spell books and broomsticks? Might this not all be some huge joke that the Carrizos had cooked up? If Roberto hadn't known that the Carrizos had no sense of humor, he might have thought so; yet somehow, even though everything Villalba had told him so far was unbelievable, Roberto couldn't help trusting him.
"This is it," said Villalba, coming to a halt, "the Leaky Cauldron. It's a famous place."
It was a tiny, grubby-looking pub. If Villalba hadn't pointed it out,
52
Roberto wouldn't have noticed it was there. The people hurrying by didn't glance at it. Their eyes slid from the big book shop on one side to the record shop on the other as if they couldn't see the Leaky Cauldron at all. In fact, Roberto had the most peculiar feeling that only he and
Villalba could see it. Before he could mention this, Villalba had steered him inside.
For a famous place, it was very dark and shabby. A few old women were sitting in a corner, drinking tiny glasses of sherry. One of them was
smoking a long pipe. A little man in a top hat was talking to the old bartender, who was quite bald and looked like a toothless walnut. The
low buzz of chatter stopped when they walked in. Everyone seemed to know Villalba; they waved and smiled at him, and the bartender reached for a glass, saying, "The usual, Villalba?"
"Can't, Oscar, I'm on Hogwarts business," said Villalba, clapping his great hand on Roberto's shoulder and making Roberto's knees buckle.
"Good Lord," said the bartender, peering at Roberto, "is this -- can this be --?"
The Leaky Cauldron had suddenly gone completely still and silent.
"Bless my soul," whispered the old bartender, "Roberto Gonzales... what an honor."
He hurried out from behind the bar, rushed toward Roberto and seized his hand, tears in his eyes.
"Welcome back, Mr. Gonzales, welcome back."
Roberto didn't know what to say. Everyone was looking at him. The old woman with the pipe was puffing on it without realizing it had gone out. Villalba was beaming.
Then there was a great scraping of chairs and the next moment, Roberto found himself shaking hands with everyone in the Leaky Cauldron.
"Doris Crockford, Mr. Gonzales, can't believe I'm meeting you at last." "So proud, Mr. Gonzales, I'm just so proud."

A pale young man made his way forward, very nervously. One of his eyes was twitching.

But the others wouldn't let Professor Quirrell keep Roberto to himself. It took almost ten minutes to get away from them all. At last, Villalba managed to make himself heard over the babble.

He had a swarthy, clever face, a pointed beard and, Roberto noticed, very long fingers and feet. He bowed as they walked inside. Now they were facing a second pair of doors, silver this time, with words engraved upon them:

Griphook held the door open for them. Roberto, who had expected more marble, was surprised. They were in a narrow stone passageway lit with flaming torches. At first they just hurtled through a maze of twisting passages. Roberto tried to remember, left, right, right, left, middle fork, right, left,

Roberto's eyes stung as the cold air rushed past them, but he kept them wide open. I never know," Roberto called to Villalba over the noise of the cart, "what's the difference between a stalagmite and a stalactite?"
"Stalagmite's got an 'm' in it," said Villalba. "An' don' ask me questions just now, I think I'm gonna be sick."
He did look very green, and when the cart stopped at last beside a small door in the passage wall, Villalba got out and had to lean against the wall to stop his knees from trembling.

a Galleon and twenty-nine Knuts to a Sickle, it's easy enough. Right,

"Hold out your arm. That's it." He measured Roberto from shoulder to finger, then wrist to elbow, shoulder to floor, knee to armpit and round his head.
